Fact	3.
Fact	The Constitution (Thirty-ninth Amendment) Act, 1975 contains three principal features.
Fact	First, Article 7 1 has been substituted by a new Article  71.
Fact	The new Article 71 states that subject to the provisions of the Constitution, <ORG> may by law regulate any matter relating to or connected with the election of a President or Vice-President including the grounds on which such election may be questioned.
Fact	4.
Fact	The second feature is insertion of Article 329A in the Constitution.
Fact	Clause 4 of Article 329A is challenged in the present appeals.
Fact	There are six clauses in Article 329A.
RulingByLowerCourt	5.
RulingByLowerCourt	The first clause states that subject to the provisions of Chapter II of Part V [except sub-clause (e) of clause (1) of Article 102] no election to either <ORG> of <ORG> of a person who holds the office of Prime Minister at the time of such election or is appointed as Prime Minister after such election; and to <ORG> of a person who holds the office of Speaker of that <ORG> at the time of such election or who is chosen as the Speaker for that <ORG> after such election; shall be called in question, except before s uch authority [not being any such authority as is referred to in clause (b) of Article 329] or body and in such manner as may be provided for by or under any law made by <ORG> and any such law may provide for all other matter relating to doubts and disputes in relation to such election including the grounds on which such election may be questioned.
Fact	6.
Fact	Under the second clause the validity of any such law as is referred to in clause (1) and the decision of any authority or body under such law shall not be called in question in any court.
Fact	7.
Fact	The third clause states that where any person is appointed as Prime Minister or, as the case may be, chosen to the office of the Speaker of <ORG>, while an election petition referred to in Article 329   (b) in respect of his election to either House of <ORG> or, as the case may be, to <ORG> is pending, such election petition shall abate upon such person being appointed as Prime Minister or, as the case may be, being chosen to t he office of the Speaker of the House of the People, but such election may be called in question under any such law as is referred to in clause (1).
Fact	8.
Fact	The fourth clause which directly concern the present appeals states that no law made by <ORG> before the commencement of the Constitution (Thirty-ninth Amendment) Act, 1975, in so far as it relates to election petitions and matters connected therewith, shall apply or shall be deemed ever to have applied to or in relation to the election of any such person as is referred to in clause (1), to either House of <ORG> and such election shall not be deemed to be void or ever to have become void on any ground on which such election could be declared to be void under any such law and notwithstanding any order made by any court, before such commencement, declaring such election to be void, such election to be void, such election continue to be valid in all respect and any such order and any finding on which such order is based shall be and shall be deemed alw ays to have been void and of no effect.
Fact	9.
Fact	The fifth clause states that any appeal or cross appeal against any such order of any court as is referred to in clause (4) pending immediately before the commencement of the Constitution (Thirty-ninth Amendment) Act, 1975, before <ORG> shall be disposed of in conformity with the provisions of clause (4).
Fact	10.
Fact	The sixth clause states that the provisions of this article shall have effect notwithstanding anything contained in the Constitution.

Precedent	33.
Precedent	The legal order is a system of general and individual norms connected with each other according to the principle that law regulates its own creation.
Precedent	Each norm of this order is created according to the provisions of another norm and ultimately according to the provisions of the basic norm constituting the unity of this system, the legal order.
Precedent	A norm belongs to a certain legal order, because it is created by an organ of the legal community constituted by this order.
Precedent	Creation of law is application of law.
Precedent	The creation of a legal norm is normally an application of the higher norm, regulating its creation.
Precedent	The application of higher norm is the creation of lower norm determined by the higher norm.
Precedent	A judicial decision is an act by w hich a general norm, a statute, is applied but at the same time an individual norm is created binding one or both parties to the conflict.
Precedent	Legislation is creation of law.
Precedent	Taking it into account is applicable of law.
Precedent	The higher norm may determine the organ and the procedure by which  34.
Precedent	The general norm which attaches abstractly determined consequences, has to be applied to concrete cases in order that the sanction determined in abstract may be ordered and executed in concrete.
Precedent	The two essential elements of judicial functions are to apply a pre-existing general norm in which a certain consequence is attached to certain conditions.
Precedent	The existence of the concrete conditions in connection with the concrete consequence are what may be called individualization of the general and abstract norm to the individual norm of the judicial decision.
Precedent	35.
Precedent	The contention is that constitutent power is an exercise in legislative process.
Precedent	The constituent power, it is said, can exercise legislative as well as judicial and executi ve powers.

Precedent	37.
Precedent	In <NAME>'s case (supra) the Special Criminal Courts (Repeal) Ordinance, 1943 which conferred validity and full effectiveness on sentences passed by special courts which functioned under the Special  Criminal Courts Ordinance, 1942 was challenged.
Precedent	It was argued in <NAME> case that the 1943 Ordinance attempted to exercise judicial power.

Precedent	<ORG> said that <ORG> simply takes up certain determinations which exist in fact, though made without authority, and prescribes not that they shall be acts done by a board of review, but that they shall be treated as they would be treated if they were such acts.
RatioOfTheDecision	The sections do not constitute an exercise of the judicial power.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> had not attempted to decide the question of the guilt or innocence of any of the accused  38.
RatioOfTheDecision	The power of the <ORG> to validate matters which have been found by judgments or orders of competent courts and tribunals to be invalid or illegal is a well-known pattern.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> validates  acts and things done by which the basis of judgments or orders of competent courts and tribunals is changed and the judgments and orders are made ineffective.
RatioOfTheDecision	All the sales tax validation cases, the election validation cases are illustrations of that proposition.
Precedent	The present appeals are not of the type of providing indemnity against penalties or determining existing facts to be treated in accordance with change of law.
Precedent	39.
Precedent	The effect of validation is to change the law so as to alter the basis of any judgment, which might have been given on the basis of old law and thus make the judgment ineffective.
Precedent	A formal declaration that the judgment rendered under the old Act is void, is not necessary.
Precedent	If the matter is pending in appeal, the appellate Court has to give effect to the altered law and reverse the judgment.
Precedent	The rendering of a judgment ineffective by changing its basis by legislative enactment is not an encroachment on judicial power but a legislation within the competence of the <ORG> rendering the b asis of the judgment non est.
Precedent	If a competent court has found that a particular tax or levy has been imposed by a law, which is void because the <ORG> passing the law was not competent to pass the law, then the competent <ORG> has validated the tax or levy by a validation Act involving a re-enactment of the invalid law.

Precedent	Judicial review is one of the distinctive features of the American Constitutional Law.

Precedent	44.
Precedent	In <NAME>'s case (supra) the <ORG> claimed additional power to disqualify a member on grounds other than those stated in the Constitution.

Fact	The <ORG> claimed that the ground on which <NAME> was disqualified was not an unconstitutional ground.

RatioOfTheDecision	The Court held that a question of unconstitutional exclu  46.
RatioOfTheDecision	Judicial review is not to be founded on any article similar to the American Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the Australian Constitution also the judicial power is located in the court.
RatioOfTheDecision	The doctrine of separation of powers is carried into effect in countries like America, Australia.
RatioOfTheDecision	In our Constitution there is separation of powers in a broad sense.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the larger question is whether there is any doctrine of separation of powers when it comes to exercise of constituent power.
RatioOfTheDecision	The doctrine of separation of powers as recognised in America is not applicable to our country.
Precedent	(See In re Delhi Laws Act ; <CITATION_1964341> ; <NAME> v. State of U. P.; and <CITATION_270610> .)
Precedent	47.
Precedent	The rigid separation of powers as under the American Constitution or under the Australian Constitution does not apply to our country.
Precedent	Many powers which are strictly judicial have been excluded from the purview of the courts.
Precedent	The whole subject of election has been left  to courts traditionally under the Common Law and election disputes and matters are governed by the <ORG>.
Precedent	The question of the determination of election disputes has particularly been regarded as a special privilege of <ORG> in England.
Precedent	It is political question in the United States.
Precedent	Under our Constitution <ORG> has inherited all the privileges, powers and immunities of <ORG> <ORG>.
Precedent	In the case of election disputes <ORG> has defined the procedure by law.
Precedent	It can at any time change that procedure and take over itself the whole question.
Precedent	There is, therefore, no question of any separation of powers being involved in matters concerning elections and election petitions.
Precedent	48.
Precedent	When the constituent power exercises powers the constituent power comprises legislative, executive and judicial powers.
Precedent	All powers flow from the constituent power through the Constitution to the various departments or heads.
Precedent	In the hands of the constituent authority there is no demarcation of powers.
Precedent	It is on ly when the constituent authority defines the authorities or demarcates the areas that separation of power is discussed.
Precedent	The constituent power is independent of the doctrine of separation of powers.
Precedent	The constituent power is sovereign.
Precedent	It is the power which creates the organs and distributes the powers.
Precedent	49.
Precedent	The constituent power is sui generis.
Precedent	It is different from legislative power.
Precedent	The position of unlimited law-making power is the criterion of legal sovereignty.
Precedent	The constituent power is sovereign because the Constitution flows from the constituent power.
Precedent	50.
Precedent	In Article 329A an exercise of judicial power is the question for determination.
Precedent	In legislative processes there may be judicial process.
Precedent	If the <ORG> has to fix the amount or lay down the principle for fixation of amount the question will arise as to whether this is exercise of judicial power.
Precedent	The determination of the amount will involve judicial procedure.
Precedent	When the <ORG> determines the amount the fixation of amount is purely by legislative  process.
Precedent	But in doing so the <ORG> takes into account factors relevant to individual properties.
Precedent	51.
RatioOfTheDecision	Every organ of the <ORG> has to ascertain facts which make the foundation of its own decision.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> usually collects its materials through its departments.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> acts in a field where there are two or more parties before it and upon evidence placed before it pronounces its verdict according to principles of natural justice.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> is entitled to obtain information from any source.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> may call witnesses.
RatioOfTheDecision	The rule of audi alteram partem is not applicable in a legislative process.
RatioOfTheDecision	Legislation is usually general.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may sometimes be for special reasons an individual case.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no doubt that the constituent power is not the same as legislative power.
Precedent	The distinction between constituent power and legislative power is always to be borne in mind because the constituent power is higher in norm.
Precedent	52.
Precedent	Judicial review in election disputes is not a compulsion.
Precedent	Judicial rev iew of decisions in election disputes may be entrusted by law to a judicial tribunal.
Precedent	If it is to a tribunal or to <ORG> the judicial review will be attracted either under the relevant law providing for appeal to this Court or Article 136 may be attracted.
Precedent	Under Article 329 (b) the contemplated law may vest the power to entertain election petitions in the <ORG> itself which may determine the dispute by a resolution after receiving a report from a special committee.
Precedent	In such cases judicial review may be eliminated without involving amendment of the Constitution.
Precedent	The Constitution permits by amendment exclusion of judicial review of a matter if it is necessary to give effect to the directive principles of <ORG> policy.
Precedent	A similar power may be available when such exclusion is needed in the larger interest of the security of the <ORG>.
Precedent	In either case the exclusion of judicial review does not mean that principles of equality are viola  53.
Precedent	Equality of status as well as equality of opportunity is a fundamenta l right in Articles 14 and 16 of the Constitution.
Precedent	It also means equality before law and equal protection of the laws.
Precedent	Equality is spoken in the Preamble.
Precedent	There is liberty to <ORG> to classify to establish equality.
Precedent	When Articles 31A and 31B eliminated judicial review the meaning was not that the <ORG> would go on discriminating.
Precedent	The task of classification can be left to the <ORG>.
Precedent	It is the very nature of legislation that classification must be in public interest.
Precedent	The amending body has excluded judicial review in Articles 31A, 31B and 31C.
Precedent	54.
Precedent	Exclusion of the operation of the equality principle from some fields is constitutionally possible.
Precedent	Article 33 excludes judicial review in matters relating to the armed forces.
Precedent	Article 262(2) excludes jurisdiction of courts in water disputes.
Precedent	55.
Precedent	Decision in election disputes may be made by the <ORG> itself or may be made by courts or tribunals on behalf of the <ORG> or may be made by courts and tribunals on their own exercising judicial  functions.
Precedent	The concept of free and fair election is worked out by the Representation of the People Act .
Precedent	The Act provides a definition of "corrupt practice" for the guidance of the court.
Precedent	In making the law the <ORG> acts on the concept of free and fair election.
Precedent	In any legislation relating to the validity of elections the concept of free and fair elections is an important consideration.
Precedent	In the process of election the concept of free and fair election is worked out by formulating the principles of franchise, and the free exercise of franchise.
Precedent	In cases of disputes as to election, the concept of free and fair election means that disputes are fairly and justly decided.
Precedent	Electoral offenses are statutory ones.

Precedent	Clause (4) in Article 329A has done four things.
Precedent	First, it has wiped out not merely the judgment but also the election petition and the law relating thereto.
Precedent	Secondly, it has deprived the right to raise a  dispute about the validity of the election by not having provided another forum.
Precedent	Third, there is no judgment to deal with and no right or dispute to adjudicate upon.
Precedent	Fourth, the constituent power of its own legislative judgment has validated the election.
Precedent	57.
Precedent	At the outset it has to be noticed that constituent power is not the same as ordinary law-making power.
Precedent	On behalf of the appellant it was rightly contended that if any amendment of Article 102(1) (e) of the Constitution had to be made, it had to be made by amendment of the Constitution.
Precedent	The matter does not rest there.
Precedent	58.
Precedent	If no law prior to the Constitution (Thirty-ninth Amendment) Act will apply to election petitions or matters connected therewith the result is that there is not only no forum for adjudication of election disputes but that there is also no election petition in the eye of law.
Precedent	The insurmountable difficulty is in regard to the process and result of validating the election by clause (4).
Precedent	Two answers were given on behalf of the appellant.
Precedent	One was that the validation of the election is itself the law.
Precedent	The other was that the constituent power applied its own norms to the election petition.
Precedent	Both the answers are unacceptable.
Precedent	If the election petition itself did not have any existence in law there was no petition which could be looked into by the constituent power.
Precedent	If there was no petition to look into it is difficult to comprehend as to what norms were applied to the election dispute.
Precedent	The dispute has to be seen.
Precedent	The dispute has to be adjudicated upon.
Precedent	59.
Argument	Clause (4) suffers from these infirmities.
Argument	First, the forum might be changed but another forum has to be created.
Argument	If the constituent power became itself the forum to decide the disputes the constituent power by repealing the law in relation to election petitions and matters connected therewith did not have any petition to seize upon to deal with the same.
Argument	Secondly, any decision is to be made in accordance with law.
Argument	<ORG> has power to create law and apply the same.
Argument	In the present case, the  constitutent power did not have any law to apply to the case, because the previous law did not apply and no other law was applied by clause (4).
Precedent	The validation of the election in the present case is, therefore, not by applying any law and it, therefore, offends rule of law.
Precedent	60.
Precedent	It is true that no express mention is made in our Constitution of vesting the <ORG> the judicial power as is to be found in the American Constitution.
Precedent	But a division of the three main functions of Government is recognised in our Constitution.
Fact	Judicial power in the sense of the judicial power of the <ORG> is vested in the <ORG>.

Precedent	62.

Precedent	The contention of the respondent is that when the power of amending the Constitution cannot be exercised to damage or destroy the basic features of the Constitution or the essential elements of the basic structure or framework thereof, the limitation on the exercise of legislative power will arise not only from the express limitations contained in the Constitution, but also from necessary implication either under articles or even in the preamble of the Constitution.
Precedent	This contention on behalf of the respondent is expanded to mean that if the democratic wa y of life through parliamentary institutions based on free and fair elections is a basic feature which cannot be destroyed or damaged by amendment of the Constitution, it cannot similarly be destroyed or damaged by any legislative measure.
Precedent	125.
Precedent	These reasons were submitted by the respondent.
RatioOfTheDecision	First, the power to resolve doubts and disputes about the validity of elections of <ORG> and State Legislatures has been vested by the Constitution in the judicial organ competent to decide election petitions and, therefore, it is not open to the legislature to take away and interfere with these exclusive functions of the <ORG> by any legislation amending the law governing the election adjudicated by the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Second, the insertion of these Acts in the Ninth Schedule will not confer any immunity on the legislative measure if basic features of the Constitution are damaged or destroyed on the ground that the provisions contravene Part III of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Third, any provision in the legislative measures w hich has the effect of bringing about unfairness between different rival candidates in the matter of election is discriminatory and it not only contravene Article 14 but also violates the implied limitation on legislative power relating to  126.
RatioOfTheDecision	The definition of "candidate" is amended by the Amendment Act , 1975, the contention of the respondent on the amendment of the definition of "candidate" are these.
RatioOfTheDecision	The expression "returned candidate" is descriptive of the person and the corrupt practices mentioned in Section 123 of the 1951 Act in relation to a candidate will not be confined to corrupt practices committed with reference to the definition of "candidate".
RatioOfTheDecision	Corrupt practices alleged in relation to candidates will be relatable to any period and will not to be confined to corrupt practices alleged between the date of nomination and the date of election.
Precedent	If corrupt practices are committed by candidates who eventually become returned candidates such corrupt practices will be offenses within the meaning of Sec tion 123 of the 1951 Act without any reference to the time of commission.
Precedent	127.
Precedent	Counsel on behalf of the respondent also contended as follows.

Precedent	Therefore, the Amendment Act , 1975 destroys and damages free and fair election by allowing candidates to commit corrupt practices prior to the date of nomination.

Precedent	The Amendment Act , 1975 is also challenged as falling within the vice of delegated legislation by the amendments inserted as Explanation 3 of Section 77 of the 1951 Act and the insertion of the proviso to Section 123(7) of the 1951 Act.

Precedent	132.
RatioOfTheDecision	The contentions on behalf of the respondent that ordinary legislative measures are subject like Constitution Amendments to the restrictions of not damaging or destroying basic structure, or basic features are utterly unsound.
RatioOfTheDecision	It  has to be appreciated at the threshold that the contention that legislative measures are subject to restrictions of the theory of basic structures or basic features is to equate legislative measures with Constitution Amendment.
Precedent	The hierarchical structure of the legal order of a <ORG> is that the Constitution is the highest level within national law.
Precedent	The Constitution in the formal sense is a solemn document containing a set of legal norms which may be changed only when special prescriptions are observed.
Precedent	The purpose of special prescriptions is to render the changes of these norms more difficult by regulating the manner and form of these amendments.
Precedent	The Constitution consists of those rules which regulate the creation of the general legal norms, in particular, the creation of statu  133.
Precedent	Articles 245 and 246 give plenary powers to <ORG> to legislate.
Precedent	The only question is whether any provision of the Constitution is violated.
Precedent	The power of plenary body is not to be construed like the power of a delegate.
Precedent	Th e largest kind of power will be attributed to <ORG>.
Precedent	The only prohibition is with reference to the provisions of the Constitution.
Precedent	The Constitution is the conclusive instrument by which powers are affirmatively created or negatively restricted.
Precedent	The only relevant test for the validity of a statue made under Article 245 is whether the legislation is within the scope of the affirmative grant of power or is forbidden by some provision of the Constitution.
Precedent	134.
Precedent	To accept the basic features or basic structures theory with regard to ordinary legislation would mean that there would be two kinds of limitations for legislative measures.
Precedent	One will pertain to legislative power under Article 245 and 246 and the legislative entries and the provision in Article 13.
Precedent	The other would be that no legislation can be made as to damage or destroy basic features or basic structures.
Precedent	This will mean rewriting the Constitution and robbing the <ORG> of acting within the framework of the Constitution.
Precedent	No legislation can be fr ee from challenge on this ground even though the legislative measure is within the plenary powers of the <ORG>.
Precedent	135.
Precedent	The theory of implied limitations on the power of amendment of the Constitution has been rejected by seven Judges in <NAME> case (supra).
RatioOfTheDecision	Our Constitution has not adopted the due process clause of the American Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Reasonableness of legislative measures is unknown to our Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The crucial point is that unlike the American Constitution where rights are couched in wide general terms leaving it to the courts to evolve necessary limitations our Constitution has denied due process as a test of invalidity of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	<CITATION_1857950> , due process was rejected by clearly limiting the rights acquired and by eliminating the indefinite due process.
RatioOfTheDecision	Our Constitution contemplates that considerations of justice or general welfare might require restriction on enjoyment of fundamental rights.
Precedent	136.
Precedent	The theory of basic structures or basic features is an exercise in imponde rables.
Precedent	Basic structures or basic features are indefinable.
Precedent	The legislative entries are the fields of legislation.
Precedent	The pith and substance doctrine has been applied in order to find out legislative competency, and eliminate encroachment on legislative entries.
Precedent	If the theory of basic structures or basic features will be applied to legislative measures it will denude <ORG> and <ORG> of the power of legislation and deprive them of laying down legislative policies.
Precedent	This will be encroachment on the separation of powers.
Precedent	137.
Precedent	The constitutional validity of a statue depends entirely on the existence of the legislative power and the express provision in Article 13.
Precedent	Apart from the limitation the <ORG> is not subject to any other prohibition.
Precedent	The amendments made to the 1951 Act by the Amendment Act s, 1974 and 1975 are to give effect to certain views expressed by this <ORG> in preference to certain views departed from or otherwise to clarify the original intention.
Precedent	It is within the powers of Parl iament to frame laws with regard to elections.
Precedent	<ORG> has power to enumerate and define election expenses.
Precedent	<ORG> has power to lay down limits on election expenses.
Precedent	<ORG> has power to state whether certain expenses can be included or may be excluded from election expenses.
Precedent	<ORG> has power to adopt conclusive proof with regard to matters of appointment, resignation or termination of service.
Precedent	<ORG> has power to state what can be considered to be office of profit.
Precedent	<ORG> has power to state as to what will and  138.
Precedent	The conclusive evidence or conclusive proof clause is an accepted legislative measure.
Precedent	Similarly, giving retrospective effect to legislative amendment is accepted to be valid exercise of legislative power.
Precedent	The well- known pattern of all validation Acts by which the basis of judgments or orders of competent courts and tribunals is changed and the judgments and orders are made ineffective is to be found in <ORG> v. <ORG> The power of the Legislatur e to pass a law includes a power to pass it retrospectively.
Precedent	An important illustration with reference to retrospective legislation in regard to election is the decision of this Court in <NAME> case (supra).
Precedent	<NAME> was disqualified by reason of holding an office of profit.
Precedent	First, the ordinance and later the Act was passed to nullify the decision of <ORG>.

Precedent	A contention was advanced that the legislative measure could not remove the disqualification retrospectively because the Constitution contemplates disqualification existing at certain time in accordance with law existing at that time.
Precedent	One of the views expressed in that case is that Article 191 recognises the power of <ORG> to declare by law that the holder of the office shall not be disqualified for being chosen as a member.
Precedent	Power is reserved to the L egislature of the <ORG> to make the declaration.
Precedent	There is nothing in the Article to indicate that this declaration cannot be made with retrospective effect.
Precedent	The Act was held not to be ineffective in its retrospective operation on the ground that it is well recognised that <ORG> and <ORG> Legislature can make their laws operate retrospectively.
Precedent	Any law that can be made prospectively can be made with retrospective operation.
Precedent	It is said that certain kinds of laws cannot operate retrospectively.
Precedent	That is ex post facto legislation.
Precedent	The  140.
Precedent	This Court in <CITATION_1588713> rejected the contention advanced there that Sections 123 (5) and 124 (5) of the 1951 Act interfered with a citizen's fundamental right to freedom of speech.
Precedent	This Court said that these laws do not stop a man from speaking.
Precedent	They merely prescribe conditions which must be observed if one wants to enter <ORG>.
Precedent	The right to stand as a candidate and to contest an election is not a common law right.
Precedent	It is a special right created by a statue wh ich can only be exercised on the conditions laid down by the statue.
Precedent	The Fundamental Rights chapter has no bearing on a right like this created by a statue relating to election.
Precedent	141.
RatioOfTheDecision	The contention on behalf of the respondent that the amendment of the definition of "candidate" has damaged or destroyed basic structure is untenable.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no basic structure or basic feature or basic framework with regard to the time when under the election laws a person is a candidate at the election.
RatioOfTheDecision	The contention of the respondent that the expression "returned candidate" is descriptive of the expression "candidate" will rob Section 100 of its content.
RatioOfTheDecision	The word "candidate" in relation to various electoral offences shows that he must be a candidate at the time of the offence.
RatioOfTheDecision	Time is necessary for fixing the offences.
Precedent	A significant distinction arises between the electoral offences under the 1951 Act and the offences under Sections 171-A to 171-I of the Indian Penal Code, namely, that the 1951 Act uses the word "candidate"  or his election agent with reference to various offences whereas the Indian Penal Code does not use the word "candidate" in relation to commission of any offence.
Precedent	Any person may  142.
Precedent	The English Representation of the People Act, 1949 called the English Act was relied on by the respondent to show that the word "candidate" in the 1951 Act should have the same meaning as in the English Act and there should be no limitation as to time in relation to a candidate.
Precedent	"Candidate" is defined in Section 103 of the English Act in relation to parliamentary election to mean a person who is elected to serve in <ORG> or a person who is nominated as a candidate at the election or is declared by himself or by others to be a candidate on or after the day of the issue of the writ for the election, or after the dissolution or vacancy in consequence of which the writ was issued.
RatioOfTheDecision	The electoral offences under the English Act speak of a person to be guilty of corrupt practices of bribery as mentioned in Section 99 , of treating a s mentioned in Section 100 and of undue influence as mentioned in Section 101 of the English Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	These sections in the English Act speak of a person and do not use the expression  143.
RatioOfTheDecision	Where a candidate is elected the English definition gives no commencing date as from which his candidature has commenced; whereas, if he be not elected, he is not a candidate until he has been nominated, or is declared to be a candidate on or after the dissolution or vacancy.
RatioOfTheDecision	A candidate who is elected is accordingly a "candidate" as soon as he has entered upon his election campaign, and has made it known that he intends to present himself as a candidate at the ensuing election, he may thus become a candidate before the dissolution of <ORG>, and may be unseated for bribery or treating committed months or even years before the vacancy or election, for such acts are offences at common law.
RatioOfTheDecision	With respect to illegal practices, which are purely statutory offences, it would seem that a narrower construction will prevail, and tha t a candidate will not be held responsible for payments etc., made before he is a candidate in point of fact, and which payments only become illegal practices by reason of his subs  144.
Precedent	It has been held in England that a candidate may be unseated for bribery of treating committed months or even years before the vacancy or election (<NAME>; <NAME>).
Precedent	The present position under the English Act is stated in <NAME>'s Conduct of Parliamentary Elections 1970 Ed.
Precedent	at p. 330 to be that since the corrupt practice under consideration is purely a statutory offence, which only becomes a corrupt practice by reason of the person in whose support the prohibited expenses were incurred subsequently becoming a candidate, the candidate may not be held responsible.
Precedent	In Norwich the question was considered in relation to the responsibility of a candidate for payments which only became illegal practices by reason of his subsequently becoming a "candidate" as defined by statute, and it was held that he was not liable.
Precedent	The liability o f a candidate under the English Act , particularly, with regard to election expenses as laid down in Section 63 of the English Act is regarded as open to doubt until the point is set  145.
Precedent	Sections 171-A to 171-I of the Indian Penal Code and the provisions contained in Sections 125 to 136 of the 1951 Act follow the pattern of English Act s, namely, Statutes 17 & 18 Victoria, Chapter CII (1853-54); Statutes 21 & 22 Victoria, Chapter LXXXVII (1858) and Statutes 46 & 47 Victoria, Chapter LI (1882).
Precedent	These English Statutes make certain acts punishable as corrupt practice when they relate to persons other than candidates or voters.
Precedent	Section II of 17 & 18 Victoria Chapter CII enacts that the persons mentioned therein shall be deemed guilty of bribery and punishable in accordance with the provisions of the Act.
Precedent	The words used there are "every person" who shall do the acts mentioned therein shall be punishable.
Precedent	In these sections dealing with the acts of persons other than candidates and voters no time is mentioned.
Precedent	On  the other hand, Section IV of Statutes 17 & 18 Victoria Chapter CII makes certain acts of voters and candidates corrupt practice.
Precedent	Section IV of the aforesaid English Statute enacts tha  146.
Precedent	The 1951 Act uses the expression "candidate" in relation to several offences for the purpose of affixing liability with reference to a person being a candidate.
Precedent	If no time be fixed with regard to a person being a candidate it can be said that from the moment a person is elected he can be said to hold himself out as a candidate for the next election.
Precedent	The definition in the English Act cannot be of any aid to the construction of the 1951 Act.
Precedent	147.
Precedent	The contention of the respondent is that if a candidate is free to spend as much as a candidate likes before the date of nomination a great premium would be placed on free use of money before the date of nomination.
Precedent	The 1951 Act specifies what election expenses are of a candidate.
Precedent	The statute specifies time in regard to a candidate.
Precedent	That time cannot be enlarged or reduced.
Precedent	The hol ding out by a person of (sic of) candidature was a flexible and elastic idea.
Precedent	The date of nomination is definite and doubtless.
Precedent	Different views may be taken as to the time of holding out.
Precedent	The <ORG> has now set the matter at rest.
Precedent	148.
Precedent	The word "incur" according to the dictionary meaning means to become liable to.
Precedent	The word "incur" means to undertake the liability even if the actual payments may not be made immediately.
Precedent	The undertaking of the responsibility for the expenditure concerned may be either by the candidate or his election agent.
Precedent	Again, a candidate is also to be deemed responsible for the expenditure if he has authorised a particular expenditure to be made by someone else on his behalf.
Precedent	149.
Precedent	The contention on behalf of the respondent is that the Amendment Act s of 1974 and 1975 fall within the vice of delegated legislation because there are no guiding principles with regard to official duty or nature of expenditure in Explanation 3 to Section 77 of the 1951 Act and in the proviso to Section 12 3 (7) of the 1951 Act.
Precedent	Official duty will be a duty in law.
Precedent	Official duty will be duty under administrative directions of the Executive.
Precedent	Official duty will be for security, law and order, and matters in aid of public purpose.
Precedent	These duties will be in connection with election.
Precedent	To illustrate, Section 197 of the Criminal Procedure Code speaks of official duty.
Precedent	150.
Precedent	This Court in <CITATION_701977> interpreted the words "official duty" to have reasonable connection between the act and the discharge of duty.
Precedent	The act must bear such relation to the duty that the person could lay a reasonable claim, but not a pretended fanciful claim, that he did it in the course of the performance of his duty.
Precedent	Where a power is conferred or a duty imposed by statute or otherwise, and there is nothing said expressly inhibiting the exercise of the power or the performance of the duty by any limitations or restrictions, it is reasonable to hold that it carries with it the power of doing all such acts or employing such means as are reason ably necessary for such execution, because it is a rule that when the law commands a thing to be done, it authorises the performance of whatever may be necessary for executing its command.
Precedent	151.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no vice of delegation in the statutes.
RatioOfTheDecision	"Delegation is not the complete handing over or transference of a power from one person or body of persons to another.
RatioOfTheDecision	Delegation may be defined as the entrusting, by a person or body of persons, of the exercise of a power residing in that person or body of persons to another person or body of persons, with complete power of revocation or amendment remaining in the grantor or delegator.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is important to grasp the implications of this, for, much confusion of thought has unfortunately resulted from assuming that delegation involves or may involve, the complete abdication or abrogation of a power.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is precluded by the definition.
RatioOfTheDecision	Delegation often involves the granting of discretionary authority to another, but such authority is purely derivative.
Precedent	The ultimate power a lways remains in the delegator and is never renounced."
Precedent	( <CITATION_603957> .)
Precedent	152.
Precedent	The Constitution Twenty-ninth Amendment Act was considered by this Court in <NAME> case (supra).
Precedent	The Twenty-ninth Amendment Act inserted in the Ninth Schedule to the Constitution Entries 65 and 66 being the Kerala Land Reforms Act, 1969 and the Kerala Land Reforms Act, 1971.
Precedent	This Court unanimously upheld the validity of the Twenty-ninth Amendment Act.
Precedent	The unanimous view of this Court in <NAME> case is that Article 31-B is not open to challenge.
Precedent	Six Judges held that the Twenty- ninth Amendment Act would be ineffective to protect the impugned Act if they took away the fundamental rights.
Precedent	Six Judges took the view that the Twenty-ninth Amendment Act is valid and further that Article 31-B has been held by this Court to be independent of Article 31-A and that Article 31-B protects scheduled Acts and Regulations and none of the scheduled Acts and Regulations is deemed to be void or ever to have become v oid on the ground of contravention of any fundamental rights.
Precedent	Article 31-B gives  153.
Precedent	The contentions of the respondent that the Amendment Act s of 1974 and 1975 are subject to basic features or basic structure or basic framework fails on two grounds.
Precedent	First, legislative measures are not subject to the theory of basic features or basic structure or basic framework.
Precedent	Second, the majority view in <NAME> case (supra) is that the Twenty-ninth Amendment which put the two statutes in the Ninth Schedule and Article 31-B is not open to challenge on the ground of either damage to or destruction of basic features, basic structure or basic framework or on the ground of violation of fundamental rights.
Precedent	154.
Precedent	The symbol allotted to the party of the appellant was characterised by the respondent as a religious symbol.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under Article 324 the superintendence, direction and control of elections to <ORG>, is vested in <ORG>.
Statute	Rule 5 of the Conduct of Elections Rules, 1961 states that the Electi on Commission shall, by notification in <ORG> and in <ORG> State, specify the symbols that may be chosen by candidates at elections in parliamentary or assembly constituencies and the restrictions to which their choice shall be subject.
Statute	Rule 10(4) of the 1961 Rules aforesaid states that at an election in a parliamentary or assembly constituency, where a poll becomes necessary, the returning officer shall consider the choice of symbols, expressed by the contesting candidates in their nomination papers and shall, subject to any general or special direction issued in this behalf by <ORG> allot a different symbol to each contesting candidate in conformi  155.
Statute	Clause 17 of the Election Symbols (Reservation and Allotment) Order, 1968 provides that the commission shall by notification in <ORG> publish lists specifying national parties and the symbols respectively reserved for them etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	There can, therefore, be no doubt that the power of evolving  permissible symbols is exclusively vested in <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is under their directions that the Returning Officer has to make allotments and allotments are made in terms of Clause 5, 6 and 8.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, in the matter of evolving of the permissible symbols, the jurisdictions is vested in <ORG>.
Precedent	If a candidate displays in addition to the allotted symbol which may have a special appeal on grounds of religion to a particular community, then the Court will be entitled to go into this question.
Precedent	156.
Precedent	With regard to the symbol of cow and calf being a religious symbol it was said on behalf of the respondent that <ORG> asked for cow, calf and milkmaid symbol and were refused.
Precedent	They were given the symbol of a "Rising Sun".
Precedent	It is impossible to hold that because one party has not been given the symbol of cow, calf and milkmaid, therefore, the symbol of cow and calf becomes a religious symbol.
Precedent	<ORG> on the evidence adduced by the respondent rightly cam e to the conclusion that there was no evidence to prove that the cow and calf is a religious symbol.
Precedent	<ORG> rightly held that cow and calf is not a religious symbol.
Precedent	157.

RatioOfTheDecision	The finding of <ORG> that the resignation of <NAME> did not become effective until it was notified in the <ORG> is also set aside because under the law the resignation became effective from January 14, 1971.
RulingByPresentCourt	The finding of <ORG> that the appellant committed corrupt practice in breach of Section 123 (7) of the 1951 Act is also repelled by the legislative changes and is, therefore, set aside.
RulingByPresentCourt	The order of the disqualification of the appellant is also set aside.

Statute	Article 329A reads as under :  "329A.
Statute	Special provision as to elections to parliament in the case of Prime Minister and Speaker.
Statute	- (1) Subject to the provisions of Chapter II of Part V [Except sub-clause (e) of clause (1) of Article 102 ], no election -  (a) to either <ORG> of a person who holds the office of Prime Minister at t he time of such election or is appointed as Prime minister after such election;  (b) to <ORG> of a person who holds the office of speaker of that <ORG> at the time of such election or who is chosen as the Speaker for that <ORG> after such election;  shall be called in question, except before such authority [not being any such authority as is referred to in clause (b) of Article 329] or body and in such manner as may be provided for by or under any law made by parliament and any such law may provide for all other matters relating to doubts and disputes in relation to such election include the grounds on which such election may be questioned.
Statute	(2) The validity of any such law as is referred to in clause (1) and the decision of any authority or body under such law shall not be called in question in any court.
Statute	(3) Where any person is appointed as Prime Minister or, as the case may be, chosen to the office of the Speaker of the <ORG> of People, while an election petition referred to in clause (b ) of Article 329 in respect of his election to either <ORG> or, as the case may be, to <ORG> is pending, such election petition shall abate upon such person being appointed as Prime Minister or, as the case may be, being chosen to the office of the Speaker of <ORG>, but such election may be called in question under any such law as is referred to in clause (1).
Statute	(4) No law made by <ORG> before the commencement of the Constitution (Thirty-ninth Amendment) Act, in so far as it relates to election petitions and matters connected therewith, shall apply or shall be deemed ever to applied to or in relation to the election of any such person as is referred to in clause (1) to either <ORG> and such election shall not be deemed to be void or ever to have become void on any ground on which such election could be declared to be void or has, before such commencement, been declared to be void under any such commencement, been declared to be void under any s uch law and notwithstanding any order made by any court, before such commencement, declaring such election to be void, such election shall continue to be valid in all respects and any such order and any finding on which such order is based shall be and shall be deemed always to have been void and of no effect.
Statute	(5) Any appeal or cross appeal against any such order of any court as is referred to in clause (4) pending immediately before the commencement of the Constitution (Thirty-ninth Amendment) Act, before <ORG> shall be disposed of in conformity with the provisions of clause (4).
Statute	(6) The provisions of this article shall have effect notwithstanding anything contained in this Constitution."

Argument	At the hearing of the appeal Mr. <NAME> on behalf of the appellant has relied upon clause (4) of the new Article 3 29A and has contended that that clause clearly applies to the present case.
Argument	It is urged that in view of that clause no law made by <ORG> before the coming into force of the Constitution (Thirty-ninth Amendment) Act, i.e., before August 10, 1975, in so far as it relates to the election petitions and matters connected therewith shall apply or shall be deemed ever to have applied to or in relation to the election to <ORG> of the appellant who being Prime Minister is one of the persons referred to in clause (1) of that article.
Argument	It is further submitted that in view of that clause, the election of the appellant shall not be deemed to be void or ever to have become void on any ground on which such election could be declared to be void or has before such commencement been declared to be void under any such law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Mr. <NAME> also adds that notwithstanding the order made by <ORG> b  173.
RatioOfTheDecision	In reply Mr. <NAME> on behalf of the respondent has not controverted, and in our opinion rightly, the stand  taken by Mr. <NAME> that clause (4) of the article applies to the facts of the present case.
Argument	He, however, contends that Section 4 of the Constitution Amendment Act which has inserted Article 329A in the Constitution is invalid.
Statute	The validity of the above constitutional amendment has been challenged by Mr. <NAME> on the following two grounds :  (1) The above constitutional amendment affects the basic structure or framework of the Constitution and is, therefore, beyond the amending power under Article 368.

RatioOfTheDecision	It is necessary to clarify at the outset that we are concerned in the present case only with the constitutional validity of clause (4) and not with that of the other clauses of that article.

RatioOfTheDecision	Clause (4) consists of four parts :  (i) No law made by <ORG> before the commencement of the Constitution (Thirty-ninth Amendment) Act in so far as it relates to the election petitions of matters connected therewith shall apply or shall be deemed ever to have applied to or in relation to the election of any such person as is referred to in clause (1) to either <ORG> of <ORG>;(ii) and such election shall not be deemed to be void or ever to have become void on any ground on which such election could be declared to be void or has before such com mencement been declared to be void under any such law;  (iii) and notwithstanding any order made by any court before such commencement declaring such election to be void, such election shall continue to be valid in all respects;  (iv) and any such order and any finding on which such order is based shall be and shall be deemed always to have been void and of no effect.
RatioOfTheDecision	186.
RatioOfTheDecision	In so far as part (i) is concerned, I find that it relates to a matter which can be the subject of an ordinary legislation or a constitutional amendment.
RatioOfTheDecision	According to this part, no law made by <ORG> before the commencement of the Constitution (Thirty-ninth Amendment) Act in so far as it relates to the election petitions and matters connected therewith shall apply and shall be deemed ever to have applied to or in relation to the election of any such person as is referred to in clause (1) to either House of <ORG>.

RatioOfTheDecision	It is well- settled that it is permissible for a <ORG> to make a law with retrospective effect.
RatioOfTheDecision	The power of a <ORG> to make a law with retrospective effect is not curtailed or circumscribed by the fact that th  187.

RulingByPresentCourt	The law under which the election of the appellant was declared to be void as a result of the amendment was also made inapplicable with retrospective effect to the dispute relating to the election of the appellant.
RulingByPresentCourt	The resultant effect of the amendment thus was that the order by which the election of the appellant was declared to be void and the finding on which such order was based were rendered to be void and no effect.
RatioOfTheDecision	189.
RatioOfTheDecision	Another aspect of part (iv) of clause (4) relates to the question as to whether it is open to <ORG> to declare an  order and a finding of <ORG> to be void and of no effect or whether such a declaration can be made only either in separate judicial proceedings before a higher court.
RatioOfTheDecision	190.
RatioOfTheDecision	A declaration that an order made by a court of law is void is normally part of the judicial function and is not a legislative function.
RatioOfTheDecision	Although there is in the Constitution of India no rigid separation of powers, by and large the spheres of judicial function and legislative function have been demarcated and it is not permissible for the <ORG> to encroach upon the judicial sphere.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has accordingly been held that a <ORG> while it is entitled to change with retrospective effect the law which formed the basis of the judicial decision, it is not permissible to the <ORG> to declare the judgment of the court to be void or not binding (see <CITATION_1018531> , <CITATION_968805> v. <ORG> and State of Tamil Nadu v. <NAME>).
RatioOfTheDecision	191.
Argument	The position as it prevails in the Unites States, wh ere guarantee of due process of law is in operation, is given on pages 318-19 of Vol.
Argument	46 of the American Jurisprudence 2d as under :  "The general rule is that the <ORG> may not destroy, annul, set aside, vacate, reverse, modify, or impair the final judgment of a court of competent jurisdiction, so as to take away private rights which have become vested by the judgment.
RatioOfTheDecision	A statute attempting to do so has been held unconstitutional as an attempt on the part of the <ORG> to exercise judicial power, and as a violation of the constitutional guarantee of due process of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> is not only prohibited from reopening cases previously decided by the courts, but is also forbidden to affect the inherent attributes of a judgment.
RatioOfTheDecision	That the statute is under the guise of an act affecting remedies does not alter the rule.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is worthy of notice, however, that there are cases in which judgments requiring acts to be done in the future may validly be affected by subsequent legislation making illegal that  which the judgment found to be legal, or making legal that which the judgment found to be illegal.
Precedent	10.
Precedent	Judgment as to public right.
Precedent	With respect to legislative interference with the judgment, a distinction has been made between public and private rights under which distinction a statute may be valid even though it renders ineffective a judgment concerning a public right.
Precedent	Even after a public right has been established by the judgment of the court, it may be annulled by subsequent legislation."
Precedent	192.

Precedent	This Court in the case of <NAME> (supra) held by majority that the power of amendment of the Constitution contained in Article 368 does not permit altering the basic structure of the Constitution.
Precedent	All the seven Judges who constituted the majority were also agreed that democratic set-up was part of the basic structure of the Constitution.
Precedent	Democracy postulates that there should be periodical election, so that people may be in a position either to re-elect the old representatives or, if they so choose, to change the representatives and elect in their place other representative.
Precedent	Democracy further contemplates that the elections should be free and fair, so that the voters may be in a position to vote for candidates of their choice.
Precedent	Democracy can indeed function only upon the faith that election are free and fair and not rigged and manipulated, that they are effective instruments of ascertaining popular will both in reality and form  and are not mere rituals calculated to generate illusion of defe ion arises whether a member of <ORG> has become disqualified for membership, the decision of that <ORG> shall be taken and shall be final.
Precedent	199.
Precedent	Not much argument is needed to show that unless there be a machinery for resolving an election dispute and for going into the allegations that elections were not free and fair being vitiated by malpractices, the provision that a candidate should not resort to malpractices would be in the nature of a mere pious wish without any legal sanction.
Precedent	It is further plain that if the validity of the election of a candidate is challenged on some grounds, the said election can be declared to be valid only if we provide a forum for going into those grounds and prescribe a law for adjudicating upon those grounds.
Precedent	If the said forum finds that the grounds advanced to challenge the election are not well-founded or are not sufficient to invalidate the election in accordance with the prescribed law or dism isses the petition to challenge the election on some other ground, in such an event it can be said that the election of the returned candidate is valid.
Precedent	200.
Precedent	Besides other things, election laws lay down a code of conduct in election matters and prescribe, what may be called, rules of electoral morality.
Precedent	Election laws also contain a provision for resolving disputes and determination of controversies which must inevitably arise in election matters as they arise in other spheres of human activity.
Precedent	The object of such a provision is to enforce rules of electoral morality and to punish deviance from the prescribed code of conduct in election matters.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is manifest that but for such a provision, there would be no sanction for the above code of conduct and rules of electoral morality.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is also plain that nothing would bring the code of conduct into greater contempt and make a greater mockery of it than the absence of a provision to punish its violation.
RatioOfTheDecision	The position would become all the more glaring that even tho ugh a provision exists on the statute book for punishing violation of the code of conduct in election matters, a particular election is made immune and g  201.
RatioOfTheDecision	The vice of clause (4) of Article 329A is not merely that it makes the previous law contained in the R. P. Act as amended by Act 58 of 1974 and 40 of 1975 inapplicable to the challenge to the election of the appellant, it also makes no other election law applicable for resolving that dispute.
RatioOfTheDecision	The further vice from which the said clause suffers is that it not merely divests the previous authority, namely, <ORG> of its jurisdiction to decide the dispute relating to the election of the appellant, it confers no jurisdiction on some other authority to decide that dispute.
RatioOfTheDecision	Without even prescribing a law and providing a forum for adjudicating upon the grounds advanced by the respondent to challenge the election of the appellant, <ORG> has declared the election of the appellant to be valid.
RatioOfTheDecision	202.
RatioOfTheDecision	To confer an absolute validity upon  the election of one particular candidate and to prescribe that the validity of that election shall not be questioned before any forum or under any law would necessarily have the effect of saying that howsoever gross may be the improprieties which might have vitiated that election, howsoever flagrant may be the malpractices which might have been committed on behalf of the returned candidate during the course of the election and howsoever foul and violative of the principles of free and fair elections may be the means which might have been employed for securing success in that election, the said election would be nonetheless valid and it would not be permissible to complain of those improprieties, malpractices and unfair means before any forum or under any law with a view to assail the validity of that election.
RatioOfTheDecision	Not much argument is needed to show that any provision which brings about that result is subversive of the principle of free and fair election in a democracy.
RatioOfTheDecision	T  203.
RatioOfTheDecision	The vice of declaration contained  in part (iii) of clause (4) regarding the validity of the election of the appellant is aggravated by the fact that such a declaration is made after <ORG> which was then seized of jurisdiction had found substance in some of the grounds advanced by the respondent and had consequently declared the election of the appellant to be void.
RatioOfTheDecision	To put a stamp of validity on the election of candidate by saying that the challenge to such an election would not be governed by any election law and that the said election in any case would be valid and immune from any challenge runs counter to accepted norms of free and fair elections in all democratic countries.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the case of <NAME> v. <NAME>, <NAME> said that "the government of the United States has been emphatically termed a government of laws and not of men".
Precedent	In United States v. <NAME>, <NAME> observed that" no man is so high that he is above the law... All... officers are creatures of the law and are bound t  204.
Precedent	It has been argued on behalf of t he appellant that the grounds on account of which the election of the appellant has been held to be void by <ORG> were of a technical nature.
Precedent	I need not express any opinion about this aspect of the matter at this stage but, assuming it to be so, I find that clause (4) of Article 329A is so worded that however serious may be the malpractices vitiating the election of the Speaker or the Prime Minister, the effect of clause (4) is that the said election would have to be treated as valid.
Precedent	I cannot accede to the submission that in construing clause (4) we should take into account the facts of the appellant's case.
Precedent	This is contrary to all accepted norms of construction.
Precedent	If a clause of a Constitution or a statutory provision is widely worded, the width of its ambit cannot be circumscribed by taking into account the facts of an individual case to which it applies.
Precedent	As already mentioned, clause (4) deals with the past election not merely of the Prime Minister but also of  205.
Precedent	Another argument advanced in supp ort of the validity of the amendment is that we should take it that <ORG> constituted itself to be the forum for deciding the dispute relating to the validity of the election of the appellant, and after considering the facts of the case, declared the election of the appellant to be valid.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is, however, nothing before us as to indicate that <ORG> went into the material which had been adduced before <ORG> relating to the validity of the election of the appellant and after considering that material held the election of the appellant to be valid.
RatioOfTheDecision	Indeed, the statement of objects and reasons appended to the Constitution (Thirty-ninth Amendment) Bill makes no mention of this thing.
Precedent	In any case, the vice of clause (4) would still lie in the fact that the election of the appellant was declared to be valid on the basis that it was not to be governed by any law for settlement of election disputes.
Precedent	Although the provisions of a c  206.
Precedent	The matter can also be looked at  from another angle.
Precedent	The effect of impugned clause (4) is to take away both the right and the remedy to challenge the election of the appellant.
Precedent	Such extinguishment of the right and remedy to challenge the validity of the election, in my opinion, is incompatible with the process of free and fair elections.
Precedent	Free and fair elections necessarily postulate that if the success of a candidate is secured in elections by means which violate the principle of free and fair elections, the election should on that account be liable to be set aside and be declared to be void.
Precedent	To extinguish the right and the remedy to challenge the validity of an election would necessarily be tantamount to laying down that even if the election of a candidate is vitiated by the fact that it was secured by flagrant violation of the principles of free and fair election, the same would still enjoy immunity from challenge and would be nonetheless valid.
Precedent	Clause (4) of Article 329A can, therefore, be held to st  207.
Precedent	I agree that it is not necessar y in a democratic set-up that disputes relating to the validity of the elections must be settled by courts of law.
Precedent	There are many countries like France, Japan, and the United States of America where consistently with the democratic set-up the determination of such controversies is by <ORG> or by authorities other than the courts.
Precedent	The question with which we are concerned, however, is whether it is permissible in a democratic set-up that a dispute with regard to the validity of a particular election shall not be raised before any forum and shall not be governed by law and whether such an election can be declared, despite the existence of a dispute relating to its validity, to be valid by making the existing law relating to election disputes not applicable to it and also by not applying any other election law to such a dispute.
Precedent	The answer to such a question, for the reasons given earlier by me, should be in the negative.
Precedent	208.
Precedent	Reference to the election of the US President made by Mr. <NAME> is also not helpfu l to him.
Precedent	It is clear from observations on pages 47-50 of the American Commonwealth by <NAME>, 1912 Ed.
Precedent	and Sections 5, 6, and 15 of the United States Code (1970 Ed.)
Precedent	that there is ample provision for the determination of such disputes after the poll.
Precedent	The fact that such determination of the dispute is before the declaration of the result would not detract from the proposition that it is essential for free and fair elections that there should be a forum and law for the settlement of such disputes relating to the validity of the election.
Precedent	209.
Precedent	Argument has also been advanced that the offices of the Prime Minister and Speaker are of great importance and as such they constitute a class by themselves.
Precedent	This argument, in my opinion, would have relevance if instead of the law governing disputes relating to the election of other persons, another law had been prescribed to govern the dispute relating to the election of a person who holds the office of the Prime Minister or Speaker.
Precedent	As it is, what we find is that so far  as the dispute relating to the election of the appellant is concerned, neither the previous law governing the election of persons holding the office of the Prime Minister is to apply to it nor the future law to be framed under clause (1) of Article 329A governing the election of persons holding the office of Prime Minister is to apply to this dispute.
Precedent	Likewise, the previous forum for adjudicating upon the election dispute which went into the matter, has been divested of its jurisdiction with retrospective effect and, at the same time,  210.
Precedent	It has been argued in support of the constitutional validity of clause (4) that as a result of this amendment, the validity of one election has been preserved.
Precedent	Since the basic structure of the Constitution, according to the submission, continues to be the same, clause (4) cannot be said to be an impermissible piece of constitutional amendment.
Precedent	The argument has a seeming plausibility about it, but a deeper reflection would show that it is vitiated by a basic fallacy.
Precedent	Law n ormally connotes a rule or norm which is of general application.
Precedent	It may apply to all the persons or class of persons or even individuals of a particular description.
Precedent	Law prescribes the abstract principles by the application of which individual cases are decided.
Precedent	Law, however, is not what <NAME> called "a sentence".
Precedent	According to <NAME>, law, as distinguished from laws, is the system of authoritative materials for grounding or guiding judicial and administrative action recognized or established in a politically organized society o be decided is that if the impugned amendment of the Constitution violates a principle which is part of the basic structure of the Constitution, can it enjoy immunity from an attack on its validity because of the fact that for the future the basic structure of the Constitution remains unaffected.
Precedent	The answer to the above question, in my opinion, should be in the negative.
Precedent	What has to be seen in such a matter is whether the amendment contravenes or runs counter to an imperative  rule or postulate which is an integral part of the basic structure of the Constitution.
Precedent	If so, it would be an impermissible amendment and it would make no difference whether it relates to one case or a large number of cases.
Precedent	If an amendment striking at the basic structure of the Constitution is not permissible, it would not acquire validity by being related only to one case.
Precedent	To accede to the amendment advanced in support of the validity of the amendment would be tantamount to holding that even though it is permissible to change the bas  211.
Precedent	Lastly, question arises whether we should strike down clause (4) in its entirety or in part.
Precedent	So far as this aspect is concerned, I am of the view that the different parts of clause (4) are so integrally connected and linked with each other that it is not possible to sever them and uphold the validity of part of it after striking down the rest of it.
Precedent	It would indeed be unfair to the appellant if we were to uphold the first part of clause (4) and strike down other parts or  even part (iii).
Precedent	As would be apparent from what follows hereafter, the election of the appellant is being upheld by applying the provisions of the R. P. Act as amended by Act 40 of 1975.
Precedent	Such a course would not be permissible if we were to uphold the validity of the first part of clause (4) and strike down the other parts.
Precedent	We would also in that event be creating a vacuum which is the very vice for which we are striking down clause (4).
Precedent	I am, therefore, of the view that clause (4) should be struck down in its entirety.
Precedent	212.
Precedent	In view of my finding that clause (4) strikes at the basic structure of the Constitution, it is not necessary to go into the question as to whether, assuming that <ORG> took it upon itself to decide the dispute relating to the validity of the election of the appellant, it was necessary for <ORG> to hear the parties concerned before it declared the election of the appellant to be valid and thus in effect repelled the challenge of the respondent to th e validity of the appellant's election.
Precedent	213.
Precedent	As a result of the above, I strike down clause (4) of Article 329A on the ground that it violates the principle of free and fair elections which is an essential postulate of democracy and which in its turn is a part of the basic structure of the Constitution inasmuch as (1) it abolishes the forum without providing for another forum for going into the dispute relating to the validity of the election of the appellant and further prescribes that the said dispute shall not be governed by any election law and the validity of the said election shall be absolute and not consequently be liable to be assailed, and (2) it extinguishes both the right and the remedy to challenge the validity of the aforesaid election.
Precedent	214.
Precedent	We may now deal with Appeal No.
Precedent	887 of 1975 filed by the appellant.
Argument	So far as this appeal is concerned, it has been argued by Mr. <NAME> on behalf of the appellant that the grounds on which the election of the appellant has been declared by <ORG> to  be void no longer hold good in view of the amendment in the R. P. Act by Act 40 of 1975.
Argument	As against that, Mr. <NAME> on behalf of the respondent has assailed the validity of Act 40 of 1975.

Precedent	215.
Precedent	The question as to whether Act 40 of 1975 is not vitiated by the constitutional infirmity would be dealt with by me subsequently.
Precedent	For the time being I would proceed upon the basis that the statutory amendment in the R. P. Act by Act 40 of 1957 is constitutionally valid.
Precedent	216.
Precedent	Section 10 of Act 40 of 1975, which has been reproduced earlier makes it clear, inter alia, that the provisions of Section 6 , 7 and 8 of the Act shall have retrospective operation, so as to apply to or in relation to any election held before the commencement of this Act, to either <ORG> of Parliament in respect of which appeal  from any order of <ORG> made in any election petition under the R. P. Act is pending before <ORG> immediately before such commencement.
Precedent	It is, therefore, obvious that the provision of Section 6 , 7 and 8 of Act 40 of 1975 would be attracted to this case.
Precedent	One of the question which actually arose for determination before <ORG> was as to what was the date on which the appellant held herself out as a candidate.
Precedent	According to the written statement filed on behalf of the appellant, she held herself out as a candidate from the Rae Bareli constituency on February 1, 1971 when she filed her nomination paper.
Precedent	As against that, the case of the respondent was that t"  unless the context otherwise requires 'candidate' means a person who has been or claims to have been duly nominated as a candidate at any election and any such person shall be deemed to have been a candidate as from the time when with the election in prospect, he began to hold himself out as a prospective candidate."
Precedent	The new de finition states that" unless the context otherwise requires, 'candidate' means a person who has been or claims to have been duly nominated as a candidate at any election ".
Precedent	The question as to when a person holds himself out as candidate, therefore, loses its importance in the context of the new definition.
Precedent	217.
Precedent	One of the grounds which weighed with <ORG> in declaring the election of the appellant to be void was that the appellant committed corrupt practice under the Section 123 (7) of the R. P. Precedent	218.
Statute	According to clause (7) of Section 123 of the R. P. Act, the following act shall constitute corrupt practice under that clause :"  The obtaining or procuring or abetting or attempting to obtain or procure by a candidate or his agent or, by any other p erson with the consent of a candidate or his election agent, any assistance (other than the giving of vote) for the furtherance of the prospects of that candidate's election, from any person in the service of the <ORG> and belonging to any of the following classes, namely :-  (a) gazetted officers;  (b) stipendiary judges and magistrates;  (c) members of the armed forces of the union;(d) members of the police forces;  (e) excise officers;  (f) revenue officer other than village revenue officers known as Lambardars, Malguzars, Deshmukhs or by any other name, whose duty is to collect land revenue and who are remunerated by a share of, or commission on, the amount of land revenue collected by them but who do not discharge any police function; and  (g) such other class of persons in the service of the <ORG> as may be prescribed.
Statute	Explanation.
Statute	- (1) In this section the expression 'agent' includes an election agent, polling agent and any person who is held to have acted as an agent in connection with the  consent of the candidate.
Statute	(2) For the purposes of clause (7), a person shall be deemed to assist in the furtherance of the prospects of a candidate's election if he acts as an election agent.
RatioOfTheDecision	"Perusal of the above clause shows that what constitutes corrupt practice under the above clause is the obtaining or procuring or abetting or attempting to obtain or procure by a candidate or his agent or by any person with the consent of a candidate or his election agent any assistance (other than giving of vote) for the furtherance of the prospects of the candidate's election from any person in the person in the service of the <ORG> and belonging to any of the classes specified therein.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is, in my opinion, essential that at the time the impugned act, namely, the obtaining or procuring or abetting or attempting to obtain or procure the assistance of the government servant is done, the person doing the act must be a candidate or his agent or any other person with the consent of a candidate or his election agent.
RatioOfTheDecision	Candidate in this clause would mean a person who has been or who claims to have been duly nominated as a candidate at the election.

RatioOfTheDecision	I find it difficult to accede to the above submission as there is nothing in the above proviso to confine the words "official duty" to duty imposed by statute.
RatioOfTheDecision	Official duty would include not merely duties imposed by statutes but also those which have to be carried out in pursuance of administrative instructions.
