Precedent	He contended   519.
Argument	Learned Counsel supporting the Thirty-ninth Amendment, had, in defence of the amendment, advanced arguments which go beyond the position which was adopted to support the amendments considered by us in <NAME> case (supra).
Argument	The new argument now advanced to use the language of the Solicitor General in his last written submissions, is that the power of amendment under Article 368 is"  the very original power of the people which is unbroken into the legislative and the executive and the judicial ".
Argument	He submitted that the implied limitations, to which the majority decision in <NAME> case (supra) has committed this <ORG> for the time being, are no longer available when considering the "unbroken" power.
Argument	Mr. <NAME>, learned Counsel for the original respondent, puts this very argument in the following words in his written submissions :"  In the hands of the constituent authority there is no demarcation of powers.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the demarcation emerges only when it leaves the hands of the con stituent authority through well defined channels into demarcated pools.
RatioOfTheDecision	The constituent power is independent of the fetters or limitations imposed by separation of powers in the hands of the organs of the <ORG> amongst whom the supreme authority of the <ORG> is allocated.
RatioOfTheDecision	The constituent power is independent of the doctrine of separation of powers.
RatioOfTheDecision	Separation of powers is when the Constitution is framed laying down the distribution of the powers in the different organs such as the legislative, executive and the judicial power.
RatioOfTheDecision	The constituent power springs as the fountainhead and partakes of sovereignty and is the power which creates the organ and distributes the powers.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, in a sense the constituent power is all-embracing and is at once judicial, executive and legislative, or in a sense super power The constituent power can also change the system of checks and balances upon which the separation of powers is based.
Precedent	"520.
Precedent	The theory advanced before us may have been designed to escape the logical  consequences of the majority view in <NAME> case (supra) which we cannot, sitting as a bench of five Judges in this <ORG>, overrule.
Precedent	The theory is, however, quite novel and has to be, I think dealt with by us.
Precedent	It postulates an undifferentiated or amorphous amalgam of bare power constituting the "constituent power".
Precedent	According to this theory, the power which constitutes does not need to be either constituted or prevented from exercising a power assigned by it already to a constituted authority.
Precedent	Hence, it is a power of a kind which is above the Constitution itself.
Precedent	If I am not mistaken, the learned Solicitor General did say the constituent power lies "outside" the Constitution.
Precedent	In other words, it is independent and above the Constitution itself because it pirates on the Constitution and can displace it with, so to say, one stroke of its exercise.
Precedent	I do not think that such an extreme theory could be supported by the cit  521.
Precedent	If we were to accept the theory indicated above, it would make it unneces sary to have a Constitution beyond one consisting of a single sentence laying down that every kind of power is vested in the constituent bodies which may, by means of a single consolidated order or declaration of law, exercise any or all of them themselves whenever they please whether such powers be executive, legislative, or judicial.
Precedent	Could this be the ambit of "constituent power" in our Constitution?
Precedent	Would such a view not defeat the whole purpose of a Constitution?
Precedent	Does the whole Constitution so crumble and melt in the crucible of constituent power that its part cannot be made out?
Precedent	Before we could accept a view which carries such drastic implications with it we will have to overrule the majority view in <NAME> case (supra).
Precedent	The majority view in that case, which is binding upon us, seemed to be that both the supremacy of the Constitution and separation of powers are parts of the basic structure of the Constitution.
Precedent	522.
Precedent	If "constituent power", by itself, is so transcendental and exceptional a s to be above the provisions of the Constitution itself, it should not, logically speaking, be bound even by the procedure of amendment prescribed by Article 368(2).
Precedent	I have not found any opinion expressed so far by any learned Judge of this Court to show that the constituent power is not bound by the need to follow the procedure laid down in Article 368(2) of the Constitution.
Precedent	Indeed, rather inconsistently with the theory of an absolute and unquestionable power in some undifferentiated or raw and unfettered form, operating from above and outside the Constitution, learned Counsel, supporting the impugned fourth clause in Article 329A , concede that the constituent power is bound by the appropriate procedure laid down in Article 368 for the amendment of the Constitution.
Precedent	What they urge is that, subject to this procedure, which has been followed here, the constituent power cannot be questioned because it is a "sovereign power".
Precedent	The log  523.
Precedent	I think that the possibly theoretical question indicated above, whateve r may be the object of raising it, does deserve to be seriously considered and answered by us because it discloses a basic misconception.
Precedent	Therefore, I propose to consider it at a length which seems to me to be justified by our need to clarify our thinking on a basic or "key" concept without a final commitment to a particular view on it.
Precedent	Clearer thinking, by examining a basic theoretical question from every conceivable angle, leads, I believe, to that openmindness which is needed by lawyers no less than by any other class today so that we may, contrary to our reputation, be responsive to the inevitable challenges of change.
Precedent	Justice <NAME> once said :"  Theory is the most important part of the dogma of law, as the architect is the most important man who takes part in the building of a house.
Precedent	"[<NAME> Collected Papers, (1921) p. 200].
Precedent	524.
Precedent	It seems to me that the words "sovereignty" and "sovereign power", used repeatedly learned Counsel defending the Thirty-ninth Amendment to describe the constituent power, sh ould, for several good reasons, be avoided, so far as possible, by lawyers who seek that clarity of thought for which precision in language is the first requirement.
Precedent	One of these reasons was given by Lord Bryce [Studies in "History and Jurisprudence"(1901) (pp.
Precedent	503-504] :"  The frontier districts, if one may call them so, of Ethics, of Law and of political science have been thus infested by a number of vague or ambiguous terms which have produced many barren discussions and caused much needless trouble to students....... No offender of this kind has given more trouble than the so-called 'Doctrine of Sovereignty'".
Precedent	Prof. <NAME>, however, opined :  " But this very fact is proof of its vital importance in our modern world, and the wide variety of the views held concerning its essence, as well as the conflicting conclusions to which these views still lead, may furnish sufficient excuse for another attempt to clarify some of our ideas touching this central formula under which we try to rationalize the complicat ed facts of our modern political life.
Precedent	"Another reason for eschewing such expressions, so far as possible, is that they are "emotive" or of a kind about which Mr. <NAME>, (writing on "Key Concepts in Political Science" Series, at p. 7) rightly observed."

RatioOfTheDecision	The term "sovereign"  is derived from the Latin word "Superanus" which was akin to "Suzerian" suggesting a hierarchy of classes which characterised ancient and medieval societies.
RatioOfTheDecision	In its origin, it is an attribute assigned to the highest living human superiors in the political hierarchy and not some abstract quality of a principle or of a law contained in a document - a meaning, as will be shown here, which emerges clearly later.

Precedent	The Greeks and Romans were not troubled by theories of "sovereignty" in a State.
Precedent	The principle that Might was Right was recognised as  the unquestioned legally operative principle at least in the field of their constitutional laws.
Precedent	Greek philosophers had, however, formulated a theory of a Law of Nature which was, morally, above the laws actually enforced.
Precedent	In later stages of Roman law, Roman jurists also, saturated with Greek notions of an ethically superior law of nature, said that the institution of slavery, which gave the owner of a slave theoretically absolute powers of life and death over the slave, just like the powers of a pater-familia over his children, was contrary to just natural although it was recognised by jus gentium, the laws of then civilised world.

Precedent	The last mentioned consisted of orders of Roman Emperors which were of various kinds, some of general application to cases of particular kinds and others for particular individual cases : Edicta, Decreta, <NAME>, Rescripta.
Precedent	They had the "force of law" or "Lex" which could be roughly equated with our statutory law.
Precedent	"Decreta" were issued as decisions on individual disputes, in exercise of the Emperor's power "under" the authority of "Lex de Imperio", although"  in the classical period it was firmly established that what the Emperor ordained had the force of law "(See : <NAME> on Roman Law, 1961, p. 32 Edn.).

Precedent	This is what we were asked to accept.
Precedent	587.
RatioOfTheDecision	The well recognised rule of construction of statutes, which must apply to the interpretation of the Constitution as well, is : "Expressio unius est exclusio alterius".
RatioOfTheDecision	From this is derived the subsidiary rule that an expressly laid down mode of doing something necessarily prohibits the doing of that thing in any other manner.
Precedent	The broad general principle is thus summarised in <NAME>'s "Statutory Constructions"(1940) at p. 334 :  "Express Mention and Implied Exclusion (Expressio unius  est exclusio alterius.
Precedent	- As a general rule, in the interpretation of statutes, the mention of one thing implies the exclusion of another thing.
Precedent	It therefore logically follows that if a statute enumerates the things upon which it is to operate, everything else must necessarily, and by implication, be excluded from its operation and effect.
Precedent	For instance, if the statute in question enumerates the matters over which a court has jurisdiction, no other matters may be included.
Precedent	Similarly, where a statute forbids the performance of certain things only those things expressly mentioned are forbidden.
Precedent	So also, if the statute directs that certain acts shall be done in a specified manner, or by certain person, their performance in any other manner than that specified, or by any other person than one of those named, is impliedly prohibited."
Precedent	588.
Precedent	It is interesting to note that in the Australian Constitution, where there is Article 49 , using language very similar to that of Article 105(3) of our Constitution, there is al so a separate but differently cast Article 47 of the Australian Constitution corresponding to Article 329(b) of our Constitution.
Precedent	This article runs as follows :  " Article 47.
Precedent	Unit the <ORG> otherwise provides, any question respecting the qualification of a senator or of a member of <ORG>, or respecting a vacancy in either <ORG> of the <ORG>, any question of a disputed election to either <ORG>, shall be determined by the <ORG> in which the question arises."
Precedent	589.
Precedent	What is separately, expressly, and especially provided for by Article 329 (b) must necessarily fall outside the purview of Article 105(3) on the principle stated above.
Precedent	Moreover, Article 105(3) contained a temporary provision until other provision was made by <ORG> in that behalf.
Precedent	Appropriate provision were enacted by the Act of 1951 in compliance with Article 329(b) because that was the proper article for it.
Precedent	It would be idle to contend that these provisions suddenly lapsed or ceased to exist as soon as Parliame nt took up consideration of the issues and the grounds of the decision on them by <ORG> to which reference is made in Article 329A(4 ), could only be a law-making power and not any other power which could conceivably fall under Article 105 , Sub- Article (3).
Precedent	Nevertheless, it was suggested, by copious references to the origin of the power of <ORG> to decide disputs relating to elections, that such a power exits in each <ORG> of our parliment as its inherent power.
Precedent	Such  590.
Precedent	The reason why <ORG> itself saw the need for entrusting to a rota of <ORG> judges, the jurisdiction at one time exercised by it directly to determine its election disputes, is found thus stated by <NAME>, quoting <NAME> "Parliamentary Practice and Procedure"(at pp.
Precedent	153-155) :  "For a considerable time after the <ORG> had obtained this jurisdiction, controverted elections were tried by committees specially nominated, composed of privy councillors and burgesses, well qualified for the d uties entrusted to them.
Precedent	But after 1672, it became an open committee, in which all who came had voices, and at length a hearing at the bar of the <ORG> was considered preferable to an inquiry by a committee.
Precedent	Here again, to use the words of Sir <NAME>,"  the partiality and injustice of the judges was soon notorious.
Precedent	Parties tried their strength........ the friends of rival candidates canvassed and maneuvered, and seats corruptly gained, were as corruptly protected or voted away.
Precedent	Such were the results of the usurpation of judicial functions by a popular body ".
Precedent	In order to remedy, if possible, these unquestionable evils, the statute 10 Geo.
Precedent	III c. 16, called from its author the Grenville Act, was passed in 1770, and the trial of election petitions transferred to a select committee of thirteen members, which it was thought would be 'a court independent of the <ORG>, though composed of its own members'.
Precedent	For a time there was a marked improvement in the decision of controverted elections.
Precedent	'But too soon it beca me evident that corruption and party spirit had not been overcome.
Precedent	Crowds now attended the ballot, as they had previously come to the vote.......... not to secure justice, but, to aid their own political friends.'
Precedent	The party, whether of the petitioner or sitting member, which attended in the greatest number inevitably had the numerical majority of names drawn for the committee, and from this list, the petitioner and sitting member struck out alternately one name until the committee was reduced to thirteen : the majority of the <ORG> was necessarily a majority of theBy these means the majority of the <ORG> continued, with less directness and certainty, and perhaps with less open scandal, to nominate their own members, as they had done before the Grenville Act.
Precedent	And for half a century, this system with slight variations of procedure was suffered to prevail.
Precedent	In 1939, however, the ballot was at length superseded by Sir <NAME> Act, committees were reduced to six members, and nominated by an impartial body.....  <ORG>.
Precedent	The same principle of selection was adhered to in later <ORG>, with additional securities for impartiality, and the committee was finally reduced to five members.
Precedent	The evil was thus greatly diminished, but still the sinister influence of party was not wholly overcome.
Precedent	In the nomination of election committees, one party or the other necessarily had a majority of one, and though these tribunals undoudtedly became far more able and judicial, their constitution and proceedings often exposed them to imputation of political bias.
Precedent	At length by the statute 31 & 32 Vict.
Precedent	c. 125, the trial of election petitions was transferred to certain of the puisne judges at <ORG>, who selected annually to form a rota for this specific purpose, and who inquire upon the spot in open court into the allegations of a petitioner, either claiming a seat, or alleging an undue return or election.
Precedent	The decision of the judge, who has power to reserve his judgment until he has consulted <ORG>  division of <ORG>, in which these proceedings are instituted, is final to all intents and purposes; <ORG> being bound to"  give the necessary directions for confirming or altering the returns or for issuing a writ for a new election, or carrying such determination into execution as circumstances may require ".
Precedent	And this abstract of the proceedings at elections of knights, citizens, and burgesses, concludes our inquiries into the laws and customs more peculiarly relative to <ORG>."

RatioOfTheDecision	I do not think that it is possible to contend, by resorting to some concept of a succession to the powers of the medieval "<ORG> of <ORG>" in England, that a judicial power also devolved upon our <ORG> through <ORG>, mentioned in Section 8 of the Indian Independence Act of 1947.

Precedent	Learned Counsel for the election-petitioner relied upon the following statement in the American Jurisprudence, 2nd Edn.
Precedent	Vol.

Precedent	A statute attempting to do so has been held unconstitutional as an attempt on the part of the <ORG> is not only prohibited from reopening cases previously decided by the courts, but is also forbidden to affect the inherent attributes of a judgment.
Precedent	That the statute is under the guise of an act affecting remedies does not alter the rule.

Precedent	"619.
Precedent	It is contended that the election of a candidate is the result of the exercise of their rights of voting by the electorate.

Precedent	Learned Counsel for the election-petitioner relied on <NAME> liyanage v. <NAME>, where <ORG> considered the validity of the Criminal Law Special amendment Act of 1962, passed by <ORG>, which had purported to legalise ex-post facto the detention of persons for having committed offenses against the <ORG>, by widening the class of offenses for which trial, without jury, by nominated judges could be ordered.

Precedent	But, as it is well established that it is the Constitution and not the constituent power which is supreme here in the sense that the constitutionality of the Constitution cannot be called in question before us, but exercise of the constituent power can be, we have to judge the validity of exercise of constituent power by testing it on the anvil of constitutional provision.
Precedent	According to the majority view in <NAME> case (supra), we can find the test primarily in the preamble to our Constitution.
Precedent	622.
Precedent	A point emphasized by <NAME> (See : "Nature & Sources  of Law" p.  96) is that unless and until courts have declared and recognised a law as enforceable it is not law at all.
Precedent	<NAME> (See : "General Theory of Law & State", p. 150) finds <NAME>'s views to be extreme.
Precedent	Courts, however, have to test the legality of laws, whether purporting to be ordinary or constitutional, by the norms laid down in the Constitution.
Precedent	This follows from the supremacy of the Constitution.
Precedent	I mention this here in answer to one of the questions set out much earlier : Does the "basic structure" of the Constitution test only the validity of a constitutional amendment or also ordinary laws?
Precedent	I think it does both because ordinary law-making itself cannot go beyond the range of constituent power.
Precedent	At this stage, we are only concerned with a purported constitutional amendment.
Precedent	According to the majority view in <NAME> case (supra) the preamble furnishes the yardstick to be applied even to constitutional amendments.
Precedent	623.
Precedent	Learned Counsel for the election-petitioner has strongly relied up on the very first purpose of the Constitution stated in the preamble to be Justice (with a capital "J") which includes "political justice".

Precedent	We are not to decide a political question here at all.

Precedent	'In all other cases', he says it is a gener al and indisputable rule, that where there is a legal right, there is also a legal remedy by suit, or action at law, whenever that right is invaded'.
Precedent	"625.
Precedent	It is true that the right which the election-petitioner claims is a purely statutory right.

Precedent	626.
Precedent	The question which arises now is : Was clause (4) of Article 329A , read with clauses (5) and (6), really meant to bar our jurisdiction to consider the grievances of the petitioner and to decide them, or, can they be so interpreted as to preserve this <ORG>'s jurisdiction?
Precedent	627.
Precedent	Broadly speaking, the election-petitioner has two heads of grievance  : firstly, that the election of the original respondent is vitiated by corrupt practices which, as I have indicated, after considering the case set up by him and the evidence tendered and the law applicable, could not possibly succeed even under the law as it stood before the amendment; and, secondly, that our very jurisdiction to go into these grievances is sought to be debarred by clauses (4), (5) and (6) of Article 329A with the political object of stifling opposition, and, therefore, according to the election-petitioner, we must declare clause (4) and the connected clauses (5) and (6) of Article 329A to be invalid.

RatioOfTheDecision	For the second set of grievances, the action complained of is that of the <ORG> itself acting through its law-making organs.

RatioOfTheDecision	It is a well established canon of interpretati on that, out of two possible interpretations of a provision, one which prevents it from becoming unconstitutional should be preferred if this is possible - ut res magis valeat quam pereat.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is true that the deeming provision seems to stand in the way of our examination of the merits of the case even though there is no direct provision taking away our jurisdiction to consider the merits of the appeals before us.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has, however, been repeatedly laid down that a deeming provision introducing a legal fiction must be confined to the context of it and cannot be given a larger effecti (See : <NAME> v. <NAME>).
RatioOfTheDecision	<CITATION_608874> , it was held by this <ORG> that a legal fiction is created for some definite purposes and should not be extended beyond its legitimate field determined by its context.
RatioOfTheDecision	The some view has been expressed by this <ORG> in other cases : C. I. T. v. <NAME>; C. I. T. v. <ORG>, Mad  633.
Precedent	In Ex parte <NAME>, In Re, <NAME>, <NAME> :"  When a statute enac ts that something shall be deemed to have been done, which in fact and in truth was not done, then the court is entitled and bound to ascertain for what purposes and between what persons the statutory fiction is to be resorted to?
Precedent	"In other words, we have to examine the context and the purpose of the legal fiction and confine its effects to these.
Precedent	634.
Precedent	If the purpose of the clause (4) of Article 329A was purely to meet the political needs of the country and was only partly revealed by the policy underlying the statement of reason and objects it seems possible to contend that it was not intended at all to out the jurisdiction of the <ORG>.
Precedent	Hence, Article 329A , clause (5) will not, so understood, bar the jurisdiction of the <ORG> to hear and decide the appeals when it says that the appeal shall be disposed of in conformity with the provision of clause (4).
Precedent	635.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the circumstances of this case, it would seem that conformity with the declaration embodied in Article 329A , clause (4) is possible, if we confi ne the meaning and effect of the deeming provision to what was needed only for the declaration to be given at the end of clause (4) by the constituent bodies, with a political object, and not for the purposes of affecting our jurisdiction which determines legal effects of what is sought to be done.
RatioOfTheDecision	Of course, the more natural interpretation would appear to be that the deeming provision should apply for "all purposes" including those for consideration of the appeals before us.
RatioOfTheDecision	But, if it is not possible to decide those appeals without giving a different meaning to the deeming provision, on which the final declaration in clause (4) rests, and clause (5) leaves us free to decide how we could conform with clause (4), need our jurisdiction to decide factual and legal issues judicially be said to be affected?
RatioOfTheDecision	If the fiction was only a logical step in the process of  636.

Precedent	The election petition out of which these appeals arise involved the question of the validity of the election of Smt.

Precedent	642.
Precedent	The defeated candidate did not lead evidence in <ORG> to show that any part of the expenditure in excess of the permissible limit of Rs.
Precedent	35, 000 was incurred by the successful candidate or her election agent.
Precedent	His contention was that the expenditure incurred for her election by the political party which had sponsored her candidature, the <ORG> (R), was liable to be included in the expenses incurred or authorised  by her.
Precedent	This contention was founded on a decision rendered by a Division Bench of this Court on October 3, 1974, in <CITATION_680015> .
Precedent	643.
Precedent	On October 19, 1974, the President of India promulgated.
Precedent	"The Representation of the People (Amendment) Ordinance, 1974" providing that :"  Notwithstanding any judgment, order or decision of any court to the contrary, any expenditure incurred or authorized in connection with the election of a candidate by a political party or by any other association or body of persons or by any individual (other than the candidate or his election agent) shall not be deemed to be, and shall not ever be deemed to have been, expenditure in connection with the election incurred or authorized by the candidate or by his election incurred or authorized by the candidate or by his election agent.....  "This provision was added by the ordinance by way of an explanation to Section 77(1) of the Representation of the People Act, 1951.
Precedent	It expressly excepted from its operation decisions of the <ORG>  Court voiding an election before the commencement of the ordinance.
Fact	<NAME> fell outside the ordinance.

Fact	645.

Fact	This Act , if valid, virtually seals the controversy into he appeal filed in this Court by the successful candidate from the decision of <ORG>.
Fact	It also takes care of a considerable gamut of the appeal filed in this Court by the defeated candidate.
Statute	It substitutes a new Section 8-A in the Representation of the People Act , 1951 empowering the President to decide whether a person found guilty of corrupt practice shall be disqualified and if so, for what period.
Statute	By Section 6 , it amends Section 77 of the Act of 1951 making pre-nomination expenses a matter of irrelevant consideration.

Fact	The amendments brought about by Act 58 of 1974 and Act 40 of 1975 have an incisive impact on the cross-appeals but their edge was blunted by the Constitution (Thirty-ninth Amendment) Act, 1975 which came into force on August 10, 1975.
Fact	The Thirty-ninth Amendment introduces two new articles in the Constitution : Article 71 and 329A; and it puts in the Ninth Schedule three Acts : (i) The Representation of the People Act , 43 of 1951; (ii) The Representation of the People (Amendment) Act , 58 of 1974; and (iii) The Election Laws (Amendments) Act, 1975, 40 of 1975.
Fact	The new Article 71 which replaces its precursor empowers the <ORG> to pass laws regulating the elections of the President and the Vice-President, including the making of provision for the decision of disputes relating to their election.
Fact	Artic le 329A has six clauses out of which the first three deal with the future election to the <ORG> of persons holding the office of Prime Minister of Speaker at the time of the election or who are appointed to these o  647.
Fact	At first flush, what remains to be decided judicially in face of the Thirty-ninth Amendment?
Fact	As an exercise of constituent power, the Thirty- ninth Amendment must reign supreme.
RatioOfTheDecision	The political sovereign having reposed its trust in the legal sovereign, the doings of <ORG> have an aura of sanctity that legal ingenuity may be powerless to penetrate.
RatioOfTheDecision	But that is an uninformed approach to field strewn with various shades of legal landmarks.
RatioOfTheDecision	648.
RatioOfTheDecision	While repelling the challenge to the First Constitutional Amendment which was passed in June 1951, this <ORG> held in <CITATION_1706770> , that the power of amendment conferred by Article 368 was not subject to any limitations, express or implied and that fundamental rights were within the sweep of the amending power.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Seventeent h Constitutional amendment passed in June 1964 was similarly upheld by a majority decision of this Court in <CITATION_1308308> , which took the view that the fundamental rights were not intended by the farmers of the Constitution to be finally and immutably settled when the Constitution was passed.
Fact	But the Seventeenth Amendment came to be challenged once again in <CITATION_21266288> .
Fact	By a majority of 6 : 5, this Court held that the Seventeenth Amendment was ultra vires the <ORG>'s power to amend the Constitution.
Fact	Five out of the six learned Judges held that Article 368 did not confer any power  649.
Fact	The decision in <NAME> case (supra) raised a debate of national dimensions as the <ORG>'s power to amend the Constitution so as to abridge or take away the fundamental rights virtually became a dead letter.
Fact	Under the majority judgment, <ORG> alone, called by virtue of a law to be passed under entry 97 of List I, could abridge or take away the fundamental rights.
Fact	The <ORG>,  in a resolve to re-affirm its powers, passed the Constitution (Twenty-fourth Amendment) Act on November 5, 1971.
Fact	By the Twenty-fourth Amendment, the <ORG> amended Articles 13 and 368 of the Constitution so as to provide that nothing contained in Article 13 shall apply to any amendment of the Constitution made under Article 368 and that notwithstanding anything in the Constitution, <ORG> may, in the exercise of its constituent power, amend by way of addition, variation or repeal any provision of the Constitution in accordance with he procedure laid down in Article 368.
Fact	As an instance of the  650.
Fact	A Bench of thirteen Judges of this Court sat to consider the constitutionality of the Twenty-fourth, Twenty-fifth and Twenty-ninth Amendments.
Fact	The eleven judgments delivered in that case are reported in <ORG> v. State of Keral, commonly known as the Fundamental Rights case.

Fact	(viii) The constituent power partakes of legislative power land can only be exercised within the highest ambit of the latter power.
Fact	Therefore, even with a two-third majority, the constituent body cannot exercise executive or judicial power.
Fact	For example, the power to appoint or dismiss a government servant or the power to declare war which are executive powers cannot be exercised by <ORG>.
Fact	Similarly, it cannot, in the guise of amending the Constitution, provide that an accused arraigned before a criminal court shall be acquitted and shall be deemed to be innocent.

Precedent	683.
Precedent	The Indian Constitution was enacted by <ORG> in the backdrop of the national struggle for independence.

RatioOfTheDecision	The American Constitution provides for a rigid separation of governmental powers into three basic divisions, the executive, legislative and judicial.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is an essential principle of that Constitution that powers entrusted to one department should not be exercised by any othe r department.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Australian constitution follows the same pattern of distribution of powers.
RatioOfTheDecision	Unlike these Constitution, the Indian Constitution does not expressly vest the three kinds of power in three different organs of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the principle of separation of powers is not a magic formula for keeping the three organs of the <ORG> within the strict confines of their functions.
RatioOfTheDecision	As observed by <NAME>, J. in his dissenting opinion in <ORG> v. <NAME>, the principle of separation of powers "is not a doctrinaire concept to be made use of with pedantic rigour.
RatioOfTheDecision	There must be sensible approximation, there must be elasticity of adjustment in response to the practical necessities of Government which cannot foresee today  685.
RatioOfTheDecision	The truth of the matter is that the existence, and the limitations on the powers of the three departments of government are due to the normal process of specialisation in governmental business which becomes more and more complex as civilization advances.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> mus t make laws, the <ORG> enforce them and the <ORG> interpret them because they have in their respective fields acquired an expertise which makes them competent to discharge their duly appointed functions.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Moghal Emperor, <NAME>, was applauded as a reformist because soon after his accession to the throne in 1605, he got a golden chain with sixty bells hung in his palace so that the common man could pull it and draw the attention of the ruler to his grievances and sufferings.
RatioOfTheDecision	The most despotic monarch in the modern world prefers to be armed, even if formally, with the opinion of his judges on the grievances of his subjects.
RatioOfTheDecision	686.
RatioOfTheDecision	The political usefulness of the doctrine of separation of powers is now widely recognised though a satisfactory definition of the three functions is difficult to evolve.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the function of the <ORG> is to make laws, not to decide cases.
RatioOfTheDecision	The British <ORG> in its unquestioned supremacy could enact a legislation for the settlement of a dispute or it could, with imp unity, legislate for the boiling of the Bishop of Rochester's cook.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> will not direct that an accused in a pending case shall stand acquitted or that a suit shall stand decreed.
RatioOfTheDecision	Princely India, in some parts, often did it.
RatioOfTheDecision	687.
RatioOfTheDecision	The reason of this restraint is not that the Indian Constitution recognizes any rigid separation of powers.
RatioOfTheDecision	Plainly, it does not.
RatioOfTheDecision	The reason is that the concentration of powers in any one organ may, by upsetting that fine balance between the three organs, destroy the fundamental premises of a democratic government to which we are pledged.
RatioOfTheDecision	Sir <NAME> says in his 'Law and Orders' (1965 Ed., p. 8) that neither in <NAME>'s analysis nor in <NAME>'s are the governmental powers conceived as the familiar trinity of legislative, executive and judicial powers.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME>'s "separation" took the form not of impassable barriers and unalterable frontiers, but of mutual restraints, or of what afterwards came to be known as "checks and balances"(p. 10).
RatioOfTheDecision	The three  organs must act in concert, not that their respective functions should not ever touch one another.
RatioOfTheDecision	If this limitation is respected and preserved,"  it is impossible for that situation to arise which <NAME> and <NAME> regarded as the eclips  688.
RatioOfTheDecision	I do not suggest that such an encroaching power will be pursued relentlessly or ruthlessly by our <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	But no Constitution can survive without a conscious adherence to its fine checks and balances.
RatioOfTheDecision	Just as <ORG> ought not to enter into problems entwined in the "political thicket", <ORG> must also respect the preserve of the courts.
RatioOfTheDecision	The principle of separation of powers is a principle of restraint which "has in it the precept, inmate in the prudence of self-preservation (even if history has not repeatedly brought it home), that discretion is the better part of valour"  .
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> have, by and large, come to check their valorous propensities.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the name of the Constitution, the <ORG> may not also turn its attention from the important task of legislati on to deciding court cases for which it lacks the expertise and the apparatus.
RatioOfTheDecision	If it gathers facts, it gathers facts of policy.
RatioOfTheDecision	If it records findings, it does so without a pleading and without framing any issues.
RatioOfTheDecision	And worst of all, if it decide  689.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG>, by clause (4) of Article 329A , has decided a matter of which the country's courts were lawfully seized.
RatioOfTheDecision	Neither more nor less.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is true, as contended by the learned Attorney-General and <NAME>, that retrospective validation is a well-known legislative process which has received the recognition of this <ORG> in tax cases, pre-emption cases, tenancy cases and variety of other matters.
RatioOfTheDecision	In fact, such validation was resorted to by the <ORG> and upheld by this <ORG> in at least four election cases, the last of them being <CITATION_335022> .
RatioOfTheDecision	But in all of these cases, what the <ORG> did was to change the law retrospectively so as to remove the reason of disqualification, leaving it to the courts to apply the amended law to the decision o f the particular case.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the instant case the <ORG> has withdrawn the application of all laws whatsoever to the disputed election and has taken upon itself to decide that the election is valid.
RatioOfTheDecision	Clause (5) commands the Supr  690.
RatioOfTheDecision	I find it contrary to the basic tenets of our Constitution to hold that the amending body is an amalgam of all powers -legislative, executive and judicial.
RatioOfTheDecision	"Whatever pleases the emperor has the force of law" is not an article of democratic faith.
Precedent	The basis of our Constitution is a well- planned legal order, the presuppositions of which are accepted by the people as determining the methods by which the functions of the government will be discharged and the power of the <ORG> shall be used.
Precedent	691.
Precedent	So much for the Thirty-ninth Amendment.
Precedent	The argument regarding the invalidity of the Representation of the People (Amendment) Act , 58 of 1974, and of the Election Laws (Amendment) Act , 1975 has, however, no substance.
Precedent	The constitutional amendments may, on the ratio of the Fundamental Rig hts case, be tested on the anvil of basic structure.
Precedent	But apart from the principle that a case is only an authority for what it decides, it does not logically follow from the majority judgment in the Fundamental Rights case that ordinary legislation must also answer the same test as a constitutional amendment.
Precedent	Ordinary laws have to answer two tests for their validity : (1) The law must be within the legislative competence of the <ORG> as defined and specified in Chapter I, Part XI of the Constitution, and (2) it must not offend against the provisions of Article 13(1) and (2) of the constitution.
Precedent	'Basic structure', by the majority judgment, is not a part of the fundamental rights nor indeed a provision of the Constitu  692.
Precedent	<NAME> thought it paradoxical that the higher power should be subject to a limitation which will not operate upon a lower power.
Precedent	There is no paradox, because certain limitations operate upon higher power for the reason that it is higher power.
Precedent	A constitutional amendment has  to be passed by a special majority and certain such amendments have to be ratified by the Legislatures of not less than one-half of the States as provided by Article 368(2).
Precedent	An ordinary legislation can be passed by a simple majority.
Precedent	The two powers, though species of the same genus, operate in different fields and are therefore subject to different limitations.
Precedent	693.
Precedent	No objection can accordingly be taken to the constitutional validity of the two impugned Acts on the ground that they damage or destroy the basic structure.
Precedent	The power to pass these Acts could be exercised retrospectively as much as prospectively.
Precedent	694.
